  146. On the question of &#34;does it work&#34; the answer is &#34;indecisive&#34;. Nerium is over-selling its effectiveness to be sure. The MD Anderson Cancer Center has already distanced itself from the findings at Nerium Biotechnology and the FDA has not approved this product (Of course its cosmetic in nature). There has been one highly-cited third-party clinical study with a very small group of people. While interesting, this is common in these type of items and may overall mean that the majority of people see no results at all. Cosmetic items have never been held to the same standard as prescribed medications, where dozens if not hundreds of third-party clinical studies are performed for the safety of patients and categorization of potential side effects. Instead this product seems to rely on word-of-mouth and grass-root tactics of hearsay to preach its results. If you'e ever played a game of &#34;telephone&#34; or &#34;grape-vine&#34; you can see how this approach can be misleading. That being said, playing devil's advocate, even if the product were to work exactly as advertised there is no mistake in its business model and the real company's goals.<br /><br />For those of you unaware Nerium is the latest economic evolution of multi-level marketing (MLM). MLM itself is what pyramid schemes have become since they were made illegal. While everything seems on the up-and-up, independent research has consistently shown that 99% of people that get involved as distributors in MLM's do not break even on their investment. 99%. And that's just money-wise. It is mathematically and physically impossible for everyone that gets involved in these practices to be successful. If something destroys 99% of participants it should be considered a pesticide. Or anti-bacterial soap. There is no half-way or part-time with this company. The carriers selling this product on sites like Amazon and the like might be trying to exploit certain loop-holes to make a profit on direct-selling to customers. Alas, you cannot fix a system that is fundamentally broken. The CEO of Nerium, one Jeff Olson, has connections with previous MLM practices and self-written help books as a lure to suck the uninitiated in. He is called the &#34;millionaire-maker&#34; and the money comes directly from those most in need of funds and goes to himself and his 1% in the &#34;Nerium family&#34;.
